<div id="review_91667261" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91667261" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91667261?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91667261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4457846152484851761">Are you familiar with women in refrigerators? Okay here's the deal: legendary comics writer Gail Simone noticed that a lot of women end up looking like this:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525023610i/25453627._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Which is to say, dead and <a href='http://www.lby3.com/wir/' title='Primary source' rel='nofollow noopener'>stuffed in a refrigerator</a> in order to give a hero some motivation. They're not humans; they're pain badges. <br/><br/>Which brings us to Titus and his daughter Lavinia, one of the archetypal fridged women, who's overtly dehumanized early in <i>Titus Andronicus,</i> Shakespeare's ur-tragedy and the answer to "What would it be like if</span>
  <span id="freeText4457846152484851761" style="display:none">Are you familiar with women in refrigerators? Okay here's the deal: legendary comics writer Gail Simone noticed that a lot of women end up looking like this:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525023610i/25453627._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Which is to say, dead and <a href="http://www.lby3.com/wir/" title="Primary source" rel="nofollow noopener">stuffed in a refrigerator</a> in order to give a hero some motivation. They're not humans; they're pain badges. <br><br>Which brings us to Titus and his daughter Lavinia, one of the archetypal fridged women, who's overtly dehumanized early in <i>Titus Andronicus,</i> Shakespeare's ur-tragedy and the answer to "What would it be like if Shakespeare wrote a slasher flick?" By Act 2 of this plot-stuffed murder pit, she'll be double-raped and her hands and tongue lopped off. She'll eventually manage to finger her attackers - well, stump them, I should say. But mostly she just suffers.<br><br>She accuses them using Ovid's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87906344" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Metamorphoses,</a> by the way. Shakespeare got this plot from its story of Tereus; when Lavinia flips to that story and shows it to her family, it's one of Shakespeare's cleverer meta moments. He's using the past to illuminate the present, and so is Lavinia. Literature gives us tools to understand our own fates, right?<br><br>I say "the past" because this is set in Roman times, when Titus, a great general who's lost 21 of 25 sons in battle, is asked to choose the successor for Caesar. He goes with Saturninus, who's correctly next in line but awful and who promptly steals his rival Bassianus's girlfriend, who happens to be Lavinia, and when one of Titus's remaining sons tries to steal her back Titus instantly murders the son for insubordination. So he starts right off with a shit decision and then kills his own son for disagreeing. This is stodgy, inflexible obedience to authority and order on a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/209393559" title="My review of Antigone" rel="nofollow noopener">Creonish scale.</a> By the way, we're still in Act 1 Scene 1 of this play, which is if nothing else not boring. <br><br><b>Now is a time to storm.</b><br><br>I mentioned that this is Shakespeare's ur-tragedy; you can see flashes of his later greats all over the place, and old Titus certainly has a lot in common with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91664049" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">King Lear.</a> You'll see someone else in Saturninus's backup bridal plan: the captured Goth queen Tamora, whose eldest son just got murdered by Titus in revenge for his own sons. She goes from captive to queen in about ten lines, and Saturninus has his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/657687655" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Lady Macbeth.</a> We're <i>still</i> in Act 1 Scene 1.<br><br>The wonderful director Julie Taymor starts her adaptation of <i>Titus</i> with a scene of a modern little boy making a horrific mess with his action figures, and that's about right. There's no subtlety to this play and little wisdom. It feels like Shakespeare's smashing his toys into each other. The rest of the play features a reverse Iago, Aaron the Moor; more lopping of limbs; meat pies; and Titus murdering <i>another</i> of his kids. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The pies are filled with Tamora's sons, the ones who raped Lavinia, which OBVIOUSLY they are, I don't know why Tamora acted surprised, and after that revelation there's this, like, murder flurry: Titus stabs Lavinia (because she was already raped anyway, I guess?) and then Tamora, Saturninus kills him, Lucius kills Saturninus, this is in like three lines. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It's all very bloody and distasteful. (But try the pies!) <br><br>It has its moments! Its austerity and harshness is sortof admirable. It's plotted very well, except for confusing and dumb Act IV. Tamora encouraging her sons to get on with raping Lavinia is a monstrous scene: "The worse to her, the better loved of me." I mean, just the audacity of it. And Aaron the Moor gets off one of Shakespeare's better "your mom" jokes, after Tamora's child with Saturninus comes out suspiciously...nonwhite.<br> <br>One of her sons: "Thou hast undone our mother."<br>Aaron: "Villain, I have done thy mother."<br><br>It's racist but it's a pretty sick burn too. <br><br>But it's so <i>simple,</i> Titus is - so simple that some Shakespeare nerds think a cowriter must have been involved. This was his very first tragedy - his sixth play overall, after a couple of comedies and the weak-ass Henry VI plays - and you can see, in Titus's clumsy struggles, the embryos of all the brilliant tragedy to come.<br><br>"Let us that have our tongues <br>Plot some device of further misery<br>To make us wondered at in time to come."<br><br>It's interesting to see. Shakespeare has already mastered drama but he's still working on psychology. But no, this is not that device that will make him wondered at in times to come. It's a bloody mess.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="4457846152484851761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_82708843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82708843" />
    <a title="Bram" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1797553-bram"><img alt="Bram" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82708843?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bram" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bram" href="/user/show/1797553-bram">Bram</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140714913" href="/book/show/116338.Titus_Andronicus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1797553-bram?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1797553-bram?shelf=the-bard">the-bard</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82708843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3355657011904187849">This one is tough to review; after a few days of reflection, I think my difficulty in assessing and/or analyzing <i>Titus Andronicus</i> lies in its straightforwardness. Iâ€™d like to be able to comment on character ambiguities, on the glimpses of hope amidst the darkness and slaughter, on Shakespeareâ€™s intentions and achievements outside of the horrifying entertainment on the surface. But Iâ€™ve got nothing, really. Even Titusâ€™s young grandson Lucius, a seemingly inconsequential character, just wants to e</span>
  <span id="freeText3355657011904187849" style="display:none">This one is tough to review; after a few days of reflection, I think my difficulty in assessing and/or analyzing <i>Titus Andronicus</i> lies in its straightforwardness. Iâ€™d like to be able to comment on character ambiguities, on the glimpses of hope amidst the darkness and slaughter, on Shakespeareâ€™s intentions and achievements outside of the horrifying entertainment on the surface. But Iâ€™ve got nothing, really. Even Titusâ€™s young grandson Lucius, a seemingly inconsequential character, just wants to enter the fray and to commence murdering Tamoraâ€™s family. The implication here, and what I imagine was the reason for including this member of the youngest generation, is that thereâ€™s a never-ending struggle of revenge that will continue spiraling out of control, colliding with new targets long after Tamora and her relatives have been slaughtered. Within the scope of the play, hope lies only in one side achieving complete victory over (i.e. elimination of) the other. But this is always a false hope, and it leaves us with no easily discernible or graspable positives at the storyâ€™s end. Titus spent his whole life fighting for peaceâ€”for the day that comes in the playâ€™s very first scene. Victory, glory, honor, recognition, relaxation. But as James Cameron (yes, <i>that</i> James Cameron) writes in his latest screenplay: <i>I thought I could be a warrior for peace. But sooner or later you have to wake up</i>. Few conflicts have granted the opportunity for true â€˜warriors for peace,â€™ and Roman vs. Goth/Titus vs. Tamora definitely isnâ€™t one of them. <br /><br /><i>Titus Andronicus</i> consists almost entirely of evil events split into three recurring phases: calculation/premeditation, the evil act itself, and the remorselessness that follows. These phases have varying lengths depending on the act in question, and the time spent on each is not irrelevant. For instance, the entire bloody sequence of events is kicked off when Titus flies through the first phase, deciding quickly and with almost no reflection (or subsequent remorse) to sacrifice and de-limb Tamoraâ€™s son. While this initial rushing is crucial to the revenge set-up, most of the brutal actions that follow take their time in each phase, and the gruesome one-upmanship cements the playâ€™s reputation for extraordinary violence and horror. Take, for example, Laviniaâ€™s rape and mutilation scene. (Horrible spoilers to follow). It is lengthily premeditated by three characters (Tamoraâ€™s lover and sons), and the levels of brutality in its execution are hard to fathom and harder to stomach. Tamora finds out about the plan at the last minute and instead of intervening, <i>she gives it her blessing</i>. The rapists then take pleasure in doing the deed <i>on top of Laviniaâ€™s husbandâ€™s murdered body</i>. And afterward they cut off Laviniaâ€™s hands and tongue so that she must live in the agony (in addition to the physical sort, presumably) of being unable to accuse and condemn her attackers. In this state, Lavinia hangs around for much of the remainder of the play. How this is portrayed on stage/film, I canâ€™t imagine. <br /><br />This is the world of <i>Titus Andronicus</i>, and the absurdity of it would be easy to dismiss if it wasnâ€™t so gut-wrenchingly twisted. Somehow itâ€™s the final phase of evil (remorselessness) thatâ€™s the most palatable, perhaps because it lends the play a non-realistic quality that allows the reader some distance from the events and characters. Everyone is so revenge-crazy and merciless as to become cartoonishly two-dimensional, like the baddie in, say, a James Cameron film. While the sociopathic premeditation and violence canâ€™t be shaken off even while attempting to â€˜not take this play seriouslyâ€™ as many critics have suggested over the years, some lightheartedness does seem allowable in the aftermath. Tamoraâ€™s Moor lover, Aaron, relishes his wickedness with a degree of gusto thatâ€™d make the most ridiculously spoofed bad guy appear morally ambivalent. And with a couple of exceptions, the rest of the cast isnâ€™t far behind. So like I said immediately upon finishing the play, I donâ€™t really want to give this one four stars because I think Iâ€™d like to not like it. But it is horribly compelling; reading it reminded me that Iâ€™m a living, feeling and (dare I say it) <i>sensitive</i> creature in a way that few authors have (McCarthy in <i>The Road</i>; Faulkner in <i>The Sound and the Fury</i>; BolaÃ±o in the fourth book of <i>2666</i>), even though this reminder came in the form of an uncomfortable bout of nauseous revulsion.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer39573443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2095198008046187317">I take a cue from Harold Bloom, who said Titus Andronicus should not be taken seriously at all, and would make the smoothest transition to film if directed by Mel Brooks. It is pure insanity, absolutely rip-roaring, inconceivable madness. Take Popeye (Titus):<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689754.jpg' width='250' height='300' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>and his arch-nemesis Bluto (Saturninus): <br/><br/><span>[image error]</span> <br/><br/>the witch from Snow White (or some other awful beast of a bitchâ€”Tamora) <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689756._SX540_.gif' width='240' height='200' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>coupled with Don Logan from Sexy Beast, but in black-face (Aaron, the moor): <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689757.jpg' width='250' height='200' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> + <span>[image error]</span> <br/><br/>as well as Yosemit</span>
  <span id="freeText2095198008046187317" style="display:none">I take a cue from Harold Bloom, who said Titus Andronicus should not be taken seriously at all, and would make the smoothest transition to film if directed by Mel Brooks. It is pure insanity, absolutely rip-roaring, inconceivable madness. Take Popeye (Titus):<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689754.jpg" width="250" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>and his arch-nemesis Bluto (Saturninus): <br><br><span>[image error]</span> <br><br>the witch from Snow White (or some other awful beast of a bitchâ€”Tamora) <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689756._SX540_.gif" width="240" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>coupled with Don Logan from Sexy Beast, but in black-face (Aaron, the moor): <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689757.jpg" width="250" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> + <span>[image error]</span> <br><br>as well as Yosemite Sam (Bassianus):<br><br><span>[image error]</span> <br><br>plus Demetrius:<br><br><span>[image error]</span> <br><br>Chiron:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689761.jpg" width="200" height="250" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Lucius:<br><br><span>[image error]</span> <br><br>Lavinia:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689763._SY540_.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Quintus and Martius:<br><br><span>[image error]</span> <br><br>and Marcus:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689765.jpg" width="230" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>and you have a cast for this Candide of tragic dramas. A rampage of terrifying morons. Bravo! Bravo! <br><br>Or as Titus himself puts it:<br><br><i>Rome is but a wilderness of tigers</i><br><br>This casts a light on the whole concept of the drama. Completely bestially savage jungle filled with the most insipid and detestable creatures on earth. Raping, murdering, fiending, lying assholes. A minstrel show of the most offensive characters.<br><br>Not only is their murder at every turn, as well as betrayal, intrigue and revenge. But everyone engaged in it is real dumb. <br><br>For a quick plot overview, Titus returns back to Rome from his wars with the Moors and sacrifices one of Tamoraâ€™s sons as an act of goodwill towards his fallen sons. In return, she has the emperorâ€™s brother killed so Titusâ€™ sons can be blamed for it, has her remaining sons rape and maim his daughter and perpetually mocks himâ€”eventually convincing him to cut off his own hand in a trick to â€œsaveâ€ his sons. Then itâ€™s back and forth, back and forth with violent revenge plots until a big, neurotic orgy of blood just kills damn near everyone but Lucius, who makes a yada-yada-yada speech about Rome being a fucked-up place where everyone can go crazy with revenge: the <i>extremity of griefs would make men mad</i>.<br><br>So imagine a place where everyone is dumb and violent. Where the princes talk like the most garrulous, uncouth frat boys using the euphemism for rape: <i>easy it is of a cut loaf to steal a shive</i>; or comparing rape to taking a slice of bread; where all it takes to convince the royalty of murder is for the murder-suspect to be found <i>near or around</i> the victim!: <i> need it be proved! You see it is apparent</i>; and when a vigilante coup is created out of an injured outfit, they arm themselves with the decapitated heads of their kin and a tongueless girl puts her fatherâ€™s severed hand into her mouth as they hunt:<br><br><i>the vow is made. Come, brother, take a head;<br>and in this hand the other will bear.<br>And Lavinia, thou shalt be employed in these arms,<br>Bear thou my hand, sweet wench, between thy teeth</i><br>	<br>Which essentially just reminded me of the absurd nutrigrain commercial where for no reason at all an entire office just turns mad with a lust for life:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689766.jpg" width="350" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380319433i/689767.jpg" width="350 height=" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JC2gIPnUCgw" rel="nofollow noopener">this might just blow your mindâ€¦</a><br> <br>And perhaps my favorite scene of all, when Titus rebukes his son Marcus for in excessive fashion for merely killing a fly:<br><br>Titus: <i>What dost thou strike at, Marcus, with thy knife?</i><br><br>Marcus: <i>At that that I have killed, my lordâ€”a fly.</i><br><br>Titus: <i>Out on thee, murderer! Thou killâ€™st my heart;<br>Mine eyes are cloyed with view of tyranny:<br>A deed of death done on the innocent<br>Becomes not Titusâ€™ brother. Get thee gone;<br>I see thou art not for my company.</i><br><br>There is a accentuated emotion here to an otherwise meaningless event:<br><br>Marcus: <i>Alas, my lord, I have but killed a fly. </i><br><br>Titus: <i>Poor harmless fly,<br>That, with his pretty buzzing melody,<br>Came here to make us merry! And thou hast killed him.</i><br><br>But then, Marcusâ€™ explanation is completely absurd:<br><br>Marcus: <i>Pardon me, sir; it was a black ill-favored fly<br>Like to the Empressâ€™ Moor. Therefore I killed him.</i><br><br>And all of a sudden this trivial crime becomes trivial pursuit:<br><br>Titus: <i>O, O, O,<br>Then pardon me for reprehending theeâ€¦<br>Give me thy knife, I will insult on him</i><br><br>They both start ransacking the dead, black fly. <br><br>This scene is comical. It is absurd. It is violent beyond the means of reason. It is madness. It is contempt for the auspices of what is right. It is exploitation. <br><br>And that is truly all that â€œShakespeareâ€â€™s play is here, <i>Exploitation!</i> It is magically irrational and terrifically brutal. You donâ€™t know who to root for. Everyone plays dirty. It gets to the point where you just have to laugh at the disgusting human nature. For instance, Aaronâ€™s speech before he is crudely executed:<br><br><i>Oft I have digged up dead men from their graves<br>and set them upright at their dear friendsâ€™ door,<br>even when their sorrows almost was forgot,<br>and on their skins, as on the bark of trees,<br>have with my knife carved in Roman letters,<br>â€˜Let not your sorrow die, though I am dead.â€™</i><br><br>How can you even begin to imagine the inhumanity! You can not. It is too vulgar. Too contemptuous. Too vile to be human. It is a cartoon. A play that is so violent, it is not meant to be taken for granted. Like dead baby-jokes or I fucked-your-mother jokes, it is a play that is meant to be taken apart from face values, ascribed a new sense of cleverness and delivered as a series of creative punchlines, all dabbled with sensationally violent overtones. Itâ€™s just a big, fat smorgasboard of graphic violence and decadent exploitations. What fun, what fun!<br></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IGiXUQ1FvGyalOHwnDg7LzfE/kJ7EdFk+Dx2WHLHFiGbR50/u1hJhs2HILnV43gw6hFbchHJPnbCCzz+xzakzQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mYrlrclROEhYDhy0+P5hkEbOzkqe3Rnzdv2D4+QNU4Mipe/Df0zNog8d3f2xJSKPmxtrevQF9uFMyslFUfzhbw==')})
  }
//]]>

Inhabiting a world engaged in a process of change, the characters find themselves locked in a series of transgressive situations that speak directly to contemporary experience and twenty-first-century critical concerns. Same-sex relationships, shifts of authority, and the destabilization of meaning all lend the play a surprising modernity, making it at once the most accessible of Lyly\'s plays and the one most frequently performed today. Designed for the student reader, Leah Scragg\'s edition offers a range of perspectives on the work. An extensive introduction locates the play in the context of the Elizabethan court, opening a window onto a kind of drama very different from that of more familiar sixteenth-century writers, such as Marlowe and Shakespeare. The latter\'s indebtedness to the play is fully documented, while detailed critical and performance histories allow an insight into the work\'s susceptibility to reinterpretation.<\/span>\n  <a A paragon of remorseless evil, Barabas befriends and betrays the Turkish invaders and native Maltese alike, incites a duel between the suitors for his daughter\'s hand, and takes lethal revenge upon a convent of nuns.Both tragedy and farce, this masterpiece of Elizabethan theater reflects the social and political complexities of its age. Christopher Marlowe\'s dramatic hybrid resonates with racial tension, religious conflict, and political intrigue â€” all of which abounded in 16th-century England. The playwright, who infused each one of his plays with cynical humor and a dark world view, draws upon stereotypes of Muslim and Christian as well as Jewish characters to cast an ironic perspective on all religious beliefs.The immediate success of The Jew of Malta on the Elizabethan stage is presumed to have influenced Marlowe\'s colleague, William Shakespeare, to draw upon the same source material for The Merchant of Venice. While locked up, the Duke of Florence spots Brancha in a window and attempts to woo her with the help of Livia, a widow. When Leantio returns he discovers that Brancha has been corrupted and no longer loves him because he lacks wealth and fortune. Isabella returns the love to her uncle but to keep their relationship a secret Livia encourages Isabella to marry the Ward, a young heir.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare">About William Shakespeare</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1586700347p3/947.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1249974034754531009">William Shakespeare (baptised 26 April 1564) was an English poet and playwright, widely regarded as the greatest writer in the English language and the world's pre-eminent dramatist. He is often called England's national poet and the "Bard of Avon" (or simply "The Bard"). His surviving works consist of 38 plays, 154 sonnets, two long narrative poems, and several other poems. His plays have been tr</span>
  <span id="freeText1249974034754531009" style="display:none">William Shakespeare (baptised 26 April 1564) was an English poet and playwright, widely regarded as the greatest writer in the English language and the world's pre-eminent dramatist. He is often called England's national poet and the "Bard of Avon" (or simply "The Bard"). His surviving works consist of 38 plays, 154 sonnets, two long narrative poems, and several other poems. His plays have been translated into every major living language, and are performed more often than those of any other playwright.<br /><br />Shakespeare was born and raised in Stratford-upon-Avon. Scholars believe that he died on his fifty-second birthday, coinciding with St Georgeâ€™s Day.<br /><br />At the age of 18 he married Anne Hathaway, who bore him three children: Susanna, and twins Hamnet and Judith. Between 1585 and 1592 he began a successful career in London as an actor, writer, and part owner of the playing company the Lord Chamberlain's Men, later known as the King's Men. He appears to have retired to Stratford around 1613, where he died three years later. Few records of Shakespeare's private life survive, and there has been considerable speculation about such matters as his sexuality, religious beliefs, and whether the works attributed to him were written by others.<br /><br />Shakespeare produced most of his known work between 1590 and 1613. His early plays were mainly comedies and histories, genres he raised to the peak of sophistication and artistry by the end of the sixteenth century. Next he wrote mainly tragedies until about 1608, including <i>Hamlet, King Lear</i>, and <i>Macbeth</i>, considered some of the finest examples in the English language. In his last phase, he wrote tragicomedies, also known as romances, and collaborated with other playwrights. Many of his plays were published in editions of varying quality and accuracy during his lifetime, and in 1623, two of his former theatrical colleagues published the <i>First Folio</i>, a collected edition of his dramatic works that included all but two of the plays now recognised as Shakespeare's.<br /><br />Shakespeare was a respected poet and playwright in his own day, but his reputation did not rise to its present heights until the nineteenth century. The Romantics, in particular, acclaimed Shakespeare's genius, and the Victorians hero-worshipped Shakespeare with a reverence that George Bernard Shaw called "bardolatry". In the twentieth century, his work was repeatedly adopted and rediscovered by new movements in scholarship and performance. His plays remain highly popular today and are consistently performed and reinterpreted in diverse cultural and political contexts throughout the world.<br /><br />According to historians, Shakespeare wrote 37 plays and 154 sonnets throughout the span of his life. Shakespeare's writing average was 1.5 plays a year since he first started writing in 1589. There have been plays and sonnets attributed to Shakespeare that were not authentically written by the great master of language and literature. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1249974034754531009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
